                    }
                }
            }

<!--Polyfills CustomEvent constructor in IE. Allows us to use events to manage race conditions in client side js-->
<script>
    (function () {
        if ( typeof window.CustomEvent === "function" ) return false;
        function CustomEvent ( event, params ) {
            params = params || { bubbles: false, cancelable: false, detail: null };
            var evt = document.createEvent( 'CustomEvent' );
            evt.initCustomEvent( event, params.bubbles, params.cancelable, params.detail );
            return evt;
        }

        CustomEvent.prototype = window.Event.prototype;

        window.CustomEvent = CustomEvent;
    })();
</script>

    <meta name="dc.title" content="Allele-defined genome of the autopolyploid sugarcane Saccharum spontaneum L."/>

    <meta name="dc.source" content="Nature Genetics 2018 50:11"/>

    <meta name="dc.format" content="text/html"/>

    <meta name="dc.publisher" content="Nature Publishing Group"/>

    <meta name="dc.date" content="2018-10-08"/>

    <meta name="dc.type" content="OriginalPaper"/>

    <meta name="dc.rightsAgent" content="journalpermissions@springernature.com"/>

    <meta name="dc.description" content="Modern sugarcanes are polyploid interspecific hybrids, combining high sugar content from Saccharum officinarum with hardiness, disease resistance and ratooning of Saccharum spontaneum. Sequencing of a haploid S. spontaneum, AP85-441, facilitated the assembly of 32 pseudo-chromosomes comprising 8 homologous groups of 4 members each, bearing 35,525 genes with alleles defined. The reduction of basic chromosome number from 10 to 8 in S. spontaneum was caused by fissions of 2 ancestral chromosomes followed by translocations to 4 chromosomes. Surprisingly, 80% of nucleotide binding site-encoding genes associated with disease resistance are located in 4 rearranged chromosomes and 51% of those in rearranged regions. Resequencing of 64 S. spontaneum genomes identified balancing selection in rearranged regions, maintaining their diversity. Introgressed S. spontaneum chromosomes in modern sugarcanes are randomly distributed in AP85-441 genome, indicating random recombination among homologs in different S. spontaneum accessions. The allele-defined Saccharum genome offers new knowledge and resources to accelerate sugarcane improvement. Sequencing of haploid sugarcane, Saccharum spontaneum, allows assembly of a prototypical version of the sugarcane chromosome set. This new reference genome will serve as a resource to accelerate sugarcane improvement."/>

Education, Fujian Agriculture and Forestry University, Fuzhou, China" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" Schatz</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff16">16</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Johns Hopkins University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0001 2171 9311, grid.21107.35, Departments of Computer Science and Biology, Johns Hopkins University, Baltimore, MD, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-David-Heckerman" data-author-popup="auth-David-Heckerman">David Heckerman</a></span><span class="u-js-hide">¬†
            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-4274-6084"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-4274-6084</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff17">17</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Microsoft Research" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0001 2181 3404, grid.419815.0, Microsoft Research, Redmond,  WA, USA" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Marie_Anne-Sluys" data-author-popup="auth-Marie_Anne-Sluys">Marie-Anne Van Sluys</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff18">18</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Universidade de S√£o Paulo" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 1937 0722, grid.11899.38, Departamento de Bot√¢nica, Instituto de Bioci√™ncias, Universidade de S√£o Paulo, S√£o Paulo, Brazil" /></span></sup>, </li><li class="c-author-list__item" itemprop="author" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Person"><span itemprop="name"><a data-test="author-name" data-track="click" data-track-action="open author" data-track-label="link" href="#auth-Glaucia_Mendes-Souza" data-author-popup="auth-Glaucia_Mendes-Souza">Glaucia Mendes Souza</a></span><span class="u-js-hide">¬†

            <a class="js-orcid" itemprop="url" href="http://orcid.org/0000-0002-9417-5789"><span class="u-visually-hidden">ORCID: </span>orcid.org/0000-0002-9417-5789</a></span><sup class="u-js-hide"><a href="#Aff1">1</a>,<a href="#Aff2">2</a><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="Fujian Agriculture and Forestry University" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 1760 2876, grid.256111.0, Fujian Agriculture and Forestry University and University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign School of Integrative Biology Joint Center for Genomics and Biotechnology, National Sugarcane Engineering Technology Research Center, Fujian Provincial Key Laboratory of Haixia Applied Plant Systems Biology, Key Laboratory of Genetics, Breeding and Multiple Utilization of Corps, Ministry of Education, Fujian Agriculture and Forestry University, Fuzhou, China" /></span><span itemprop="affiliation" itemscope="itemscope" itemtype="http://schema.org/Organization" class="u-visually-hidden"><meta itemprop="name" content="University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign" /><meta itemprop="address" content="0000 0004 1936 9991, grid.35403.31, Department of Plant Biology, University of Illinois at Urbana-Champaign, Urbana, IL, USA" /></span></sup>¬†</li></ul>

            <div class="c-article-body">
                <section aria-labelledby="Abs1" data-title="Abstract" lang="en"><div class="c-article-section" id="Abs1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Abs1">Abstract</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Abs1-content"><p>Modern sugarcanes are polyploid interspecific hybrids, combining high sugar content from <i>Saccharum officinarum</i> with hardiness, disease resistance and ratooning of <i>Saccharum spontaneum</i>. Sequencing of a haploid <i>S. spontaneum</i>, AP85-441, facilitated the assembly of 32 pseudo-chromosomes comprising 8 homologous groups of 4 members each, bearing 35,525 genes with alleles defined. The reduction of basic chromosome number from 10 to 8 in <i>S. spontaneum</i> was caused by fissions of 2 ancestral chromosomes followed by translocations to 4 chromosomes. Surprisingly, 80% of nucleotide binding site-encoding genes associated with disease resistance are located in 4 rearranged chromosomes and 51% of those in rearranged regions. Resequencing of 64 <i>S. spontaneum</i> genomes identified balancing selection in rearranged regions, maintaining their diversity. Introgressed <i>S. spontaneum</i> chromosomes in modern sugarcanes are randomly distributed in AP85-441 genome, indicating random recombination among homologs in different <i>S. spontaneum</i> accessions. The allele-defined <i>Saccharum</i> genome offers new knowledge and resources to accelerate sugarcane improvement.</p></div></div></section>
                <noscript>
                    
                </noscript>

                    <section aria-labelledby="Sec1" data-title="Main"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec1-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec1">Main</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec1-content"><p>Cultivated sugarcanes (<i>Saccharum</i> spp., Poaceae) are unusual among leading crops in that they are polyploid interspecific hybrids, with singularly complex genomes. Domesticated in New Guinea ~10,000 years ago, ‚Äòreeds that produce honey without bees‚Äô were considered a luxury and an expensive spice from the sixth to fourth centuries <span class="u-small-caps">bc</span>. After the introduction of sugarcane to the Old World around the eighth century<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 1" title="Watson, A. M. Agricultural Innovation in the Early Islamic World: The Diffusion of Crops and Farming Techniques, 700‚Äì1100 (Cambridge Univ. Press, 2008)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR1" id="ref-link-section-d3291e1953">1</a></sup>, its spread to Caribbean, South American, Indian Ocean and Pacific island nations drove large human migrations, including slave labor<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 2" title="Mintz, S. W. Sweetness and Power: The Place of Sugar in Modern History (Penguin, 1986)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR2" id="ref-link-section-d3291e1957">2</a></sup>. Now the world‚Äôs number one crop by harvested tonnage and its fifth most valuable crop (FAO, 2012), sugarcane is cultivated on ~26 million hectares of land in &gt;90 countries, and 1.83 billion metric tonnes are harvested annually with a gross production value approaching $57 billion, providing 80% of the world‚Äôs sugar and 40% of its ethanol as the primary sugar and biofuel feedstock crop.</p><p>While the high sugar content of modern sugarcane cultivars derives from cultivated ‚Äònoble‚Äô forms of <i>Saccharum officinarum</i>, their hardiness, disease resistance and ratooning capacity were obtained during ‚Äònobilization‚Äô, specifically backcrossing into <i>S. officinarum</i> selected traits from a sugar-poor relative, <i>Saccharum spontaneum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 3" title="Roach, B. T. Nobilization of sugarcane. Proc. Int. Soc. Sugar Cane Technol. 14, 206‚Äì216 (1972)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR3" id="ref-link-section-d3291e1972">3</a></sup>. ‚ÄòNoble‚Äô <i>S. officinarum</i> cultivars, typically 2<i>n</i>‚Äâ=‚Äâ8<i>x</i>‚Äâ=‚Äâ80 (<i>n</i> is the haploid chromosome number in eukaryotic organisms and <i>x</i> is the monoploid chromosome number in polyploid organisms), accumulate sucrose in the stem reaching up to 50% of the dry weight but are vulnerable to biotic and abiotic stresses. Dutch breeders in Java made interspecific crosses between <i>S. officinarum</i> and a wild relative, <i>S. spontaneum</i>, to obtain disease resistance and stress tolerance traits of <i>S. spontaneum</i> while backcrossing to <i>S. officinarum</i> to recover high biomass and high sugar content<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 4" title="Brandes, E. W. &amp; Sartoris, G. B. Sugarcane: its origin and improvement. Yearb. U.S. Dep. Agric. 1936, 561‚Äì624 (1936)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR4" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2005">4</a></sup>. Consequently, modern sugarcane cultivars are interspecific hybrids with approximately 80% chromosomes from <i>S. officinarum</i>, 10‚Äì15% chromosomes from <i>S. spontaneum</i>, and 5‚Äì10% recombinant chromosomes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 5" title="D‚ÄôHont, A. et al. Characterisation of the double genome structure of modern sugarcane cultivars (Saccharum spp.) by molecular cytogenetics. Mol. Gen. Genet. 250, 405‚Äì413 (1996)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR5" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2015">5</a></sup>.</p><p>The lowest chromosome number recorded for natural <i>Saccharum</i> accession is a 2<i>n</i>‚Äâ=‚Äâ5<i>x</i>‚Äâ=‚Äâ40 <i>S. spontaneum</i> that no longer exists; however, exactly one haploid (1<i>n</i>‚Äâ=‚Äâ4<i>x</i>‚Äâ=‚Äâ32) <i>S. spontaneum</i>, AP85-441, generated from a culture of octoploid SES208<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 6" title="Moore, P. H., Nagai, C. &amp; Fitch, M. M. M. Production and evaluation of sugarcane haploids. Proc. Int. Soc. Sugar Cane Technol. 20, 599‚Äì607 (1989)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR6" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2044">6</a></sup>, provides a foundation for assembly of a prototypical version of the sugarcane chromosome set. This study illuminates the hereditary blueprint and evolutionary history of one of our most important, and most complex, crop genomes.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec2" data-title="Results"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec2-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec2">Results</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec2-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec3">Genome sequencing and assembly</h3><p>The genome size of AP85-441 was estimated at 3.36‚ÄâGbp by flow cytometry<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 7" title="Zhang, J. et al. Genome size variation in three Saccharum species. Euphytica 185, 511‚Äì519 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR7" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2060">7</a></sup>. From a BAC library of AP85-441, 35,156 BAC clones were pooled into 712 libraries (mostly of 48 BACs; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">1</a>), and individual BAC pools were sequenced independently by a Hiseq 2500 with PE250 (paired-end model and 250-bp read length), yielding 267.5‚ÄâGbp of data that were assembled using three different assemblers: ALLPATHS-LG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Gnerre, S. et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 1513‚Äì1518 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2067">8</a></sup>, SPAdes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Bankevich, A. et al. SPAdes: a new genome assembly algorithm and its applications to single-cell sequencing. J. Comput. Biol. 19, 455‚Äì477 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2071">9</a></sup> and SOAPdenovo2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. GigaScience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2075">10</a></sup>, yielding a 2.56-Gbp assembly with contig N50 of 7.4‚Äâkb (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">2</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM3">3</a>). To reduce fragmentation, 295‚ÄâGbp of data from the Pacific Biosciences (PacBio) RS II system (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">4</a>) were used for self-correction and assembly by Canu<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722‚Äì736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2089">11</a></sup>, incorporating assembled BAC sequences and correcting and polishing with 90√ó Illumina paired-end sequences, yielding 3.13‚ÄâGbp with contig N50 of 45‚Äâkb (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">5</a>). The hybrid assembled contigs and BAC contigs correspond with ~99.72% accuracy (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">6</a>).</p><p>High-throughput chromatin conformation capture (Hi-C) is an extension of chromosome conformation capture (3C) technology, in which cross-linked chromatin is digested with an appropriate restriction enzyme and then ligated to obtain an interacting fragment<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 12" title="Dekker, J. The three ‚ÄòC‚Äô s of chromosome conformation capture: controls, controls, controls. Nat. Methods 3, 17‚Äì21 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR12" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2102">12</a></sup>. This approach, which was pioneered by Lieberman-Aiden et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 13" title="Lieberman-Aiden, E. et al. Comprehensive mapping of long-range interactions reveals folding principles of the human genome. Science 326, 289‚Äì293 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR13" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2106">13</a></sup> and Burton et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119‚Äì1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2110">14</a></sup>, was used previously in grasses in the assemblies of barley<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 15" title="Mascher, M. et al. A chromosome conformation capture ordered sequence of the barley genome. Nature 544, 427‚Äì433 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR15" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2114">15</a></sup> and wild emmer wheat<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 16" title="Avni, R. et al. Wild emmer genome architecture and diversity elucidate wheat evolution and domestication. Science 357, 93‚Äì97 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR16" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2118">16</a></sup>. To provide a scaffold for contig assembly, four Hi-C libraries were constructed from young leaves of AP85-441. Chimeric fragments representing the original cross-linked long-distance physical interactions were processed into paired-end sequencing libraries, then 1 billion 150-bp paired-end Illumina reads were produced and uniquely mapped onto the draft assembly contigs. Due to polyploidy, existing Hi-C scaffolding programs such as LACHESIS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 14" title="Burton, J. N. et al. Chromosome-scale scaffolding of de novo genome assemblies based on chromatin interactions. Nat. Biotechnol. 31, 1119‚Äì1125 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR14" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2123">14</a></sup> and SALSA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 17" title="Ghurye, J., Pop, M., Koren, S. &amp; Chin, C.-S. Scaffolding of long read assemblies using long range contact information. BMC Genomics 18, 527 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR17" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2127">17</a></sup> link <i>S. spontaneum</i> allelic haplotypes together and are no longer suitable for this autopolyploid genome. We developed a Hi-C-based scaffolding algorithm (ALLHIC) that integrates four functions‚Äîpruning, partition, optimization and building‚Äîto select contigs specific for polyploid genome assembly (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="section anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#Sec15">Online Methods</a> and Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">1</a>‚Äì<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">3</a>). The quality of Hi-C sequencing was evaluated using HiC-Pro<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 18" title="Servant, N. et al. HiC-Pro: an optimized and flexible pipeline for Hi-C data processing. Genome Biol. 16, 259 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR18" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2144">18</a></sup> (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">7</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">4</a>).</p><p>A Hi-C-based physical map was used to assemble 32 pseudo-chromosomes that anchor 2.9‚ÄâGbp of the genome, including 97% of the gene content. A high-density genetic map of 998,370 SNPs was used to verify the Hi-C assembly, supporting that the two methods are consistent in both chromosomal assignment and order for 89% of contigs (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">8</a>). The 32 pseudo-chromosomes comprise 8 homologous groups with 4 sets of monoploid chromosomes: A, B, C and D (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#Fig1">1</a>). A total of 219 (88.3 %) complete gene models among 248 ultraconserved core eukaryotic genes (CEGs) in CEGMA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 19" title="Parra, G., Bradnam, K. &amp; Korf, I. CEGMA: a pipeline to accurately annotate core genes in eukaryotic genomes. Bioinformatics 23, 1061‚Äì1067 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR19" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2163">19</a></sup> and 1,397 (97.01 %) among 1,440 conserved genes in BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210‚Äì3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2167">20</a></sup> were recalled in our assembly (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">9</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">10</a>). Further, 1,624 million (97.01%) of 1,674 million Illumina short reads were alignable and covered 97.3% of the assembly (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">11</a>). The assembly allowed us to predict 28 potential centromeric regions along the 32 chromosomes, with length ranging from 0.25 to 11.85‚ÄâMbp (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">12</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-1" data-title="Alignment of S. spontaneum AP85-441 chromosomes with sorghum chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig1" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 1: Alignment of <i>S. spontaneum</i> AP85-441 chromosomes with sorghum chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2/figures/1" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0237-2/MediaObjects/41588_2018_237_Fig1_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-1-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0237-2/MediaObjects/41588_2018_237_Fig1_HTML.png" alt="figure1" loading="lazy" width="685" height="637" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-1-desc"><p>A set of 4 homologous chromosomes aligned to a single sorghum chromosome. The reduction of basic chromosomes from 10 to 8 in <i>S. spontaneum</i> is caused by chromosome fissions followed by translocations of two ancestral chromosomes homologous to sorghum chromosomes 5 and 8. Inset: sorghum stratum SSA, which is mirrored in the alignment of SsChr5 to SbChr08 and SsChr7 to SbChr05 at the tip of the short arm. Inset reproduced from ref. <sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Wang, X., Tang, H. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Seventy million years of concerted evolution of a homoeologous chromosome pair, in parallel, in major Poaceae lineages. Plant Cell 23, 27‚Äì37 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2203">25</a></sup>, <a href="http://www.plantcell.org">http://www.plantcell.org</a>, Copyright American Society of Plant Biologists. Mya, million years ago.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2/figures/1" data-track-dest="link:Figure1 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec4">Allele-specific annotation</h3><p>A high level of homologous gene retention was detected from sequencing multiple haplotypes in sugarcane, despite extreme autopolyploid redundancy<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 21" title="Garsmeur, O. et al. High homologous gene conservation despite extreme autopolyploid redundancy in sugarcane. New Phytol. 189, 629‚Äì642 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR21" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2230">21</a></sup>. In autopolyploid genomes, homologous genes at the same locus on homologous chromosomes are defined as alleles<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 22" title="Osborn, T. C. et al. Understanding mechanisms of novel gene expression in polyploids. Trends Genet. 19, 141‚Äì147 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR22" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2234">22</a></sup>. Using two rounds of MAKER followed by manual annotation to separate genes and alleles, we annotated 35,525 genes with alleles defined, including 4,289 (12.7%) genes with four alleles, 9,792 (27.6%) with three, 14,797 (41.7%) with two, and 6,647 (18.7%) with one. The total number of alleles was 82,773, with an average 2.3 alleles per gene. In unanchored sequences, 3,130 gene/alleles were annotated. We annotated 1,256 tandemly duplicated genes and 3,375 dispersedly duplicated paralogs (Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="table anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#Tab1">1</a>). The cytochrome P450 gene families illustrated the importance of annotating alleles in polyploid genomes, with a total of 1,465 manually annotated alleles in 387 genes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">5</a>). Without allele-specific annotation, the number of P450 genes in this genome would be 1,465, not 387.</p><div class="c-article-table" data-test="inline-table" data-container-section="table" id="table-1"><figure><figcaption class="c-article-table__figcaption"><b id="Tab1" data-test="table-caption">Table 1 Allele annotation in the AP85-441 genome</b></figcaption><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="table-link" data-track="click" data-track-action="view table" data-track-label="button" rel="nofollow" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2/tables/1"><span>Full size table</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Among the predicted gene models, 90.0% could be found in the sorghum genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 23" title="Paterson, A. H. et al. The Sorghum bicolor genome and the diversification of grasses. Nature 457, 551 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR23" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2811">23</a></sup> and 80% in collinear positions. Comparison with rice, sorghum, maize and <i>Arabidopsis</i> (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">6</a>) showed that among 21,661 gene families, 1,278 (6%) were unique to <i>S. spontaneum</i>. Gene Ontology (GO) enrichment analysis showed that these <i>S. spontaneum</i>-specific genes were enriched in a list of GO categories, including response to wounding/external stimulus, serine-type endopeptidase/peptidase inhibitor activity and ribosomal subunit (both false discovery rate (FDR) and <i>P</i>‚Äâ&lt;‚Äâ0.01, Fisher‚Äôs exact test; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">13</a>).</p><p>AP85-441 contains 1,842‚ÄâMbp of repetitive sequences, accounting for 58.65% of the assembled genome (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">14</a>). Long terminal repeat (LTR) retrotransposons account for 45.62% of the genome, including 14.19% Ty1/<i>copia</i> and 26.04% Ty3/<i>gypsy</i>. Kimura distances analysis indicated a more recent LTR burst (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">7</a>), including Ty1/<i>copia</i> and Ty3/<i>gypsy</i> superfamilies that occurred between 0.72 and 2.9 million years ago.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec5">Basic chromosome number reduction</h3><p>The AP85-441 genome assembly showed chromosome reduction from 10 to 8 in <i>S. spontaneum</i> to involve a paleo-duplicated pair of chromosomes that have experienced frequent recombinations. Alignment to sorghum showed chromosome fissions in ancestral homologs of sorghum chromosomes 5 and 8, paleo-duplicated chromosome pairs A5 and A11 in grasses (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#Fig2">2</a>). The ancestor of SbChr05 (A12) split into two major segments, C5S (A12S) and C5L (A12L)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Salse, J. et al. Identification and characterization of shared duplications between rice and wheat provide new insight into grass genome evolution. Plant Cell 20, 11‚Äì24 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2870">24</a></sup>, that translocated into ancestors of SbChr06 (A2) and SbChr07 (A5), respectively (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#Fig2">2c</a>, event 1). The ancestor of SbChr08 (A11) split into two major segments, C8S (A11S) and C8L (A11L), and translocated into ancestors of SbChr09 (A6) and SbChr02 (A7‚Äâ+‚ÄâA9), respectively. The short fragments that appear to be homologous between SbChr08 and SsChr5 and between SbChr05 and SsChr7 are remains of homeologous genes in sorghum stratum SSA formed 13.4 million years ago, well before sorghum and <i>Saccharum</i> diverged<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 25" title="Wang, X., Tang, H. &amp; Paterson, A. H. Seventy million years of concerted evolution of a homoeologous chromosome pair, in parallel, in major Poaceae lineages. Plant Cell 23, 27‚Äì37 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR25" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2881">25</a></sup>. Strikingly, even the smaller SSA region in S5 and the larger SSA region in S8 were conserved in the rearranged AP85-441 genome, reflected in the sparse alignment of SsChr5 to SbChr08 and the dense alignment of SsChr7 to SbChr05 at the tip of the short arm (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#Fig1">1</a>), validating the high quality and accuracy of the AP85-441 genome assembly.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-2" data-title="Evolutionary history of S. spontaneum chromosomes."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig2" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 2: Evolutionary history of <i>S. spontaneum</i> chromosomes.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2/figures/2" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0237-2/MediaObjects/41588_2018_237_Fig2_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-2-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0237-2/MediaObjects/41588_2018_237_Fig2_HTML.png" alt="figure2" loading="lazy" width="685" height="797" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-2-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Evolution of chromosome numbers in Poaceae, from <i>n</i>‚Äâ=‚Äâ10 in sorghum to <i>n</i>‚Äâ=‚Äâ8 in <i>S. spontaneum</i>. Chromosomes are represented with color codes to illuminate the evolution of segments from a common ancestor with 5 chromosomes. Ancestral genomes are labeled with A<i>X</i> (<i>X</i> is a number from 1 to 12) according to Salse et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 24" title="Salse, J. et al. Identification and characterization of shared duplications between rice and wheat provide new insight into grass genome evolution. Plant Cell 20, 11‚Äì24 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR24" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2922">24</a></sup>. The <i>S. spontaneum</i> rearranged chromosomes are marked with dashed boxes. The inversion events that occurred in chromosome segments are shown with red curved arrows, and the chromosomes recombined with inverted chromosome segments are marked with red plus signs. <b>b</b>, Genomic alignments between Ss2, Ss5, Ss6 and Ss7 and Sb02, Sb05, Sb06, Sb07, Sb08 and Sb09 are shown; the inverted regions are marked with red ribbons. <b>c</b>, The genome duplications and rearrangements in <i>S. spontaneum</i>, <i>Miscanthus</i> (genetic map), and sorghum are shown. 1, The basic chromosome number reduction from 10 to 8 in <i>S. spontaneum</i> as described in the text. 2, Two inversions occurred after the first round of WGD as shown by pairs of inversions in SsChr2AB and SsChr7AB. 3, Three chromosomal fragments in SsChr6ABD are in an inverted position. 4, The ancestral chromosome SbChr07 (A5) fused into SbChr04 (A4) after an allopolyploidization event in <i>Miscanthus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Swaminathan, K. et al. A framework genetic map for Miscanthus sinensis from RNAseq-based markers shows recent tetraploidy. BMC Genomics 13, 142 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2947">27</a></sup>. 5, Four chromosomal fragments in SsChr4ABCD are in an inverted position. This is a sorghum-specific inversion in SbChr04 (A4), because the orientation of this chromosomal fragment is the same in rice, <i>Miscanthus</i>, and <i>S. spontaneum</i>.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2/figures/2" data-track-dest="link:Figure2 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec6">Polyploidization in <i>S. spontaneum</i>
                        </h3><p>We assessed whether the two rounds of whole-genome duplication (WGD) affecting sugarcane were allopolyploidization followed by autopolyploidization, as proposed<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 26" title="Kim, C. et al. Comparative analysis of Miscanthus and Saccharum reveals a shared whole-genome duplication but different evolutionary fates. Plant Cell 26, 2420‚Äì2429 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR26" id="ref-link-section-d3291e2977">26</a></sup>, or just two rounds of autopolyploidization. Although the sequenced genome is haploid, each gamete contains four sets of homologous or hom(e)ologous chromosomes, representing two WGDs (that is, from one to two to four). Comparison among hom(e)ologous haplotypes A, B, C and D revealed 7.7 million SNPs, 1.03 million short indels and 3,637 structural variations (SVs), accounting for 11.2‚ÄâMbp of sequence and indicating heterozygosity of 0.98% in the <i>S. spontaneum</i> AP85-441 genome (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#Fig3">3</a> and Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">15</a>). To exploit the fact that paralogs are often located on all four of a set of hom(e)ologous chromosomes, we developed a framework to carry out a more sensitive study of gene pair similarities (<a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a>). However, no clear partition reflecting two events could be inferred, with each of three complementary approaches suggesting random association among the four members of most homologous series.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-3" data-title="Distribution of genomic features along the sugarcane monoploid genome."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig3" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 3: Distribution of genomic features along the sugarcane monoploid genome.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2/figures/3" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0237-2/MediaObjects/41588_2018_237_Fig3_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-3-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0237-2/MediaObjects/41588_2018_237_Fig3_HTML.png" alt="figure3" loading="lazy" width="685" height="663" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-3-desc"><p>The rings indicate (from outermost to innermost) monoploid genome in Mbp (<b>a</b>), SNP density among haplotypes (<b>b</b>), gene density (<b>c</b>), expression (<b>d</b>) and nucleotide diversity (<b>e</b>). HA, HB, HC and HD indicate four haplotypes in ring B, respectively, and these four haplotypes were ordered from outside to inside in rings C, D and E.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2/figures/3" data-track-dest="link:Figure3 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>It is clear, however, from comparisons of chromosomal rearrangements that there were two discrete WGDs, rather than a single event. Two fissions in ancestral homologs of sorghum chromosomes SbChr05 and SbChr08 that resulted in translocations to a set of two chromosomes each occurred before the two rounds of WGDs in <i>Saccharum</i> and after the divergence of <i>Saccharum</i> and <i>Miscanthus</i> (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#Fig2">2c</a>). However, inversions in ancestral SbChr08S and SbChr08L in two pairs, SsChr2AB and SsChr7AB, indicate that these occurred after the first WGD but before the second (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#Fig2">2c</a>, event 2). Among three regions showing collapsing of homologous sequences (upper region of SsChr1C, middle region of SsChr3D and upper region of SsChr8C), SsChr3B and SsChr8A have about 2√ó greater depth of Illumina short reads, suggesting that they are the collapsed homologs. The SsChr1C region showed equal distribution among three homologs, indicating a deletion in SsChr1C (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">16</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">8</a>).</p><p>Two inversions involving single chromosomes, ancestral SbChr05L (A12L) (homologous chromosome C of SsChr6) (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#Fig2">2c</a>, event 3) and the bottom of SsChr5C (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#Fig2">2c</a>, event 3), presumably occurred after the two rounds of WGD. Chromosome reduction in <i>Miscanthus</i> was caused by fusion of one set of chromosomes homologous to SbChr04 and SbChr07<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 27" title="Swaminathan, K. et al. A framework genetic map for Miscanthus sinensis from RNAseq-based markers shows recent tetraploidy. BMC Genomics 13, 142 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR27" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3066">27</a></sup>. Inversions involving all four homologous chromosomes between SsChr4ABCD appear to have occurred before the two rounds of WGD, but it is actually an inversion that occurred in SbChr04 after <i>Saccharum</i> and <i>Sorghum</i> diverged from a common ancestor (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#Fig2">2c</a>, event 5). These analyses indicate that the two rounds of WGD are autopolyploidization and that they occurred with a brief time in between.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec7">Allelic expression dominance</h3><p>The homologous genome expression levels of the four homologous genomes were similar in examined tissues (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">9</a>), indicating no significant global homologous genome dominance in <i>S. spontaneum</i>. To mitigate differential gene content among the homologous genomes, we further extracted 4,289 sets of genes with four alleles in high collinearity from AP85-441 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">10</a>), but overall gene expression level from each haplotype was similar for the four homologous genomes (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">9</a>). These results are not surprising‚Äîeven recent allopolyploids such as <i>Brassica napus</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 28" title="Chalhoub, B. et al. Early allopolyploid evolution in the post-Neolithic Brassica napus oilseed genome. Science 345, 950‚Äì953 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR28" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3103">28</a></sup>, <i>Gossypium hirsutum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 29" title="Said, J. I. et al. A comparative meta-analysis of QTL between intraspecific Gossypium hirsutum and interspecific G. hirsutum‚Äâ√ó‚ÄâG. barbadense populations. Mol. Genet. Genom. 290, 1003‚Äì1025 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR29" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3110">29</a></sup>, <i>Triticum aestivum</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 30" title="International Wheat Genome Sequencing Consortium (IWGSC). A chromosome-based draft sequence of thehexaploid bread wheat (Triticum aestivum) genome. Science 345, 1251788 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR30" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3116">30</a></sup> and <i>Brassica juncea</i><sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 31" title="Yang, J. et al. The genome sequence of allopolyploid Brassica juncea and analysis of differential homoeolog gene expression influencing selection. Nat. Genet. 48, 1225‚Äì1232 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR31" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3122">31</a></sup> displayed no homologous chromosome dominance. <i>S. spontaneum</i> is a recent autopolyploid, and homologous genomes are fluid and changing after each meiosis.</p><p>For breeding polyploid crops such as sugarcane, the segregation of alleles with different expression levels may contribute to the segregation of traits in a breeding population. To simplify the analysis of the allelic expression, we sorted the expression levels of four alleles for 4,289 genes in descending order from I to IV. Three allele pairs (I/II, II/III and III/IV) were compared for analyzing the differentially expressed alleles (Supplementary Figs. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">11</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">12</a>). We defined the pairs with less than twofold difference in expression within a pair as neutral and others as non-neutral. Of 4,289 genes, on average, 37.6% displayed neutral expression and 62.4% displayed non-neutral expression, suggesting that the expression of alleles varied. We further analyzed the variations in gene number among tissues; the numbers of both neutral and non-neutral genes were similar among the examined tissues. However, the genes of these two expressional patterns varied among examined tissues. On average, 36.3% of the neutral and 56.4% of the non-neutral genes were conserved across all the examined tissues (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">13</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec8">Nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide phosphate-malic enzyme-type C<sub>4</sub> pathway</h3><p>The C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis pathway was discovered in sugarcane<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 32" title="Kortschak, H. P., Hartt, C. E. &amp; Burr, G. O. Carbon dioxide fixation in sugarcane leaves. Plant Physiol. 40, 209‚Äì213 (1965)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR32" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3154">32</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 33" title="Hatch, M. D. &amp; Slack, C. R. Photosynthesis by sugar-cane leaves. A new carboxylation reaction and the pathway of sugar formation. Biochem. J. 101, 103‚Äì111 (1966)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR33" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3157">33</a></sup>. We identified 24 genes for 7 key enzymes related to the nicotinamide adenine dinucleotide phosphate-malic enzyme (NADP-ME) C<sub>4</sub> pathway (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">14</a>). Increased expression of core C<sub>4</sub> enzymes played a major role in the evolution of C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 34" title="Sage, R. F. The evolution of C4 photosynthesis. New Phytol. 161, 341‚Äì370 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR34" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3171">34</a></sup>. Based on gene expression and phylogenetic analysis, 8 genes‚Äî<i>SsCA1</i>, <i>SsCA2</i>, <i>SsPEPC1</i>, <i>SsPEPC-k1</i>, <i>SsNADP-MDH2</i>, <i>SsNADP-ME2</i>, <i>SsPPDK1</i> and <i>SsPPDK-RP2</i>‚Äîwere identified as C<sub>4</sub>-type genes (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM4">17</a>). A tandem duplication of <i>SsNADP-ME2</i>, <i>SsNADP-ME1</i>, also displayed a C<sub>4</sub> expression profile similar to that of <i>SsNADP-ME2</i>. But the ortholog of <i>SsNADP-ME1</i> in maize, <i>ZmNADP-ME</i> (GRMZM2G122479), displayed non-C<sub>4</sub>-type expression<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 35" title="Wang, L. et al. Comparative analyses of C4 and C3 photosynthesis in developing leaves of maize and rice. Nat. Biotechnol. 32, 1158‚Äì1165 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR35" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3226">35</a></sup>, suggesting that neofunctionalization of <i>SsNADP-ME1</i> for C<sub>4</sub> in sugarcane occurred after the divergence of maize and tribe Andropogoneae.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec9">Sugar transporters</h3><p>Sucrose transporters (SUTs) are hypothesized to load sucrose into the phloem of leaf minor veins and also function to retrieve sucrose from the apoplasm during transport<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Julius, B. T., Leach, K. A., Tran, T. M., Mertz, R. A. &amp; Braun, D. M. Sugar transporters in plants: new insights and discoveries. Plant Cell Physiol. 58, 1442‚Äì1460 (2017)." href="#ref-CR36" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3243">36</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Baker, R. F. et al. Sucrose transporter ZmSut1 expression and localization uncover new insights into sucrose phloem loading. Plant Physiol. 172, 1876‚Äì1898 (2016)." href="#ref-CR37" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3243_1">37</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 38" title="Slewinski, T. L. &amp; Braun, D. M. Current perspectives on the regulation of whole-plant carbohydrate partitioning. Plant Sci. 178, 341‚Äì349 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR38" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3246">38</a></sup>. In the step prior to phloem loading, SWEETs (sugars will eventually be exported transporters) are potentially responsible for sucrose efflux into the cell wall space from phloem parenchyma cells and the bundle sheath<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 39" title="Williams, L. E., Lemoine, R. &amp; Sauer, N. Sugar transporters in higher plants‚Äîa diversity of roles and complex regulation. Trends Plant. Sci. 5, 283‚Äì290 (2000)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR39" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3250">39</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 40" title="Chen, L. Q. et al. Sucrose efflux mediated by SWEET proteins as a key step for phloem transport. Science 335, 207‚Äì211 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR40" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3253">40</a></sup>. SWEETs play various important roles in multiple physiological processes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 41" title="Eom, J. S. et al. SWEETs, transporters for intracellular and intercellular sugar translocation. Curr. Opin. Plant Biol. 25, 53‚Äì62 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR41" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3257">41</a></sup>. In sugarcane and sweet sorghum, the stems are the principal sink tissues that store very high concentrations of sugars within the parenchyma cells<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Bihmidine, S., Hunter, C. T., Johns, C. E., Koch, K. E. &amp; Braun, D. M. Regulation of assimilate import into sink organs: update on molecular drivers of sink strength. Front. Plant Sci. 4, 177 (2013)." href="#ref-CR42" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3261">42</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" title="Patrick, J. W., Botha, F. C. &amp; Birch, R. G. Metabolic engineering of sugars and simple sugar derivatives in plants. Plant. Biotechnol. J. 11, 142‚Äì156 (2013)." href="#ref-CR43" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3261_1">43</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 44" title="Bihmidine, S., Baker, R. F., Hoffner, C. &amp; Braun, D. M. Sucrose accumulation in sweet sorghum stems occurs by apoplasmic phloem unloading and does not involve differential sucrose transporter expression. BMC Plant Biol. 15, 186 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR44" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3264">44</a></sup>. Tonoplast sugar transporters (TSTs) have been characterized as sucrose transporters highly associated with vacuolar sucrose accumulation from sugar beet taproot<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 45" title="Jung, B. et al. Identification of the transporter responsible for sucrose accumulation in sugar beet taproots. Nat. Plants 1, 14001 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR45" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3268">45</a></sup>, sugarcane<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Casu, R. E. et al. Tissue-specific transcriptome analysis within the maturing sugarcane stalk reveals spatial regulation in the expression of cellulose synthase and sucrose transporter gene families. Plant Mol. Biol. 89, 607‚Äì628 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3273">46</a></sup> and sweet sorghum stems<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Bihmidine, S., Julius, B. T., Dweikat, I. &amp; Braun, D. M. Tonoplast sugar transporters (SbTSTs) putatively control sucrose accumulation in sweet sorghum stems. Plant Signal. Behav. 11, e1117721 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3277">47</a></sup> and watermelon fruit<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 48" title="Ren, Y. et al. A tonoplast sugar transporter underlies a sugar accumulation QTL in watermelon. Plant Physiol. 176, 836‚Äì850 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR48" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3281">48</a></sup>. Whereas there are 3 TST genes in the sorghum genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Bihmidine, S., Julius, B. T., Dweikat, I. &amp; Braun, D. M. Tonoplast sugar transporters (SbTSTs) putatively control sucrose accumulation in sweet sorghum stems. Plant Signal. Behav. 11, e1117721 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3285">47</a></sup>, the family has expanded in the <i>S. spontaneum</i> genome, which has 4 genes consisting of 13 homologs. Hence, it is reasonable to hypothesize that TSTs are the most promising players to sequester sucrose into the vacuoles of the sugarcane stem<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Casu, R. E. et al. Tissue-specific transcriptome analysis within the maturing sugarcane stalk reveals spatial regulation in the expression of cellulose synthase and sucrose transporter gene families. Plant Mol. Biol. 89, 607‚Äì628 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3292">46</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 47" title="Bihmidine, S., Julius, B. T., Dweikat, I. &amp; Braun, D. M. Tonoplast sugar transporters (SbTSTs) putatively control sucrose accumulation in sweet sorghum stems. Plant Signal. Behav. 11, e1117721 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR47" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3295">47</a>,<a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 49" title="Julius, B. T., Leach, K. A., Tran, T. M., Mertz, R. A. &amp; Braun, D. M. Sugar transporters in plants: new insights and discoveries. Plant Cell Physiol. 58, 1442‚Äì1460 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR49" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3298">49</a></sup>.</p><p>In the <i>S. spontaneum</i> genome, we identified 123 sugar transporters from 9 subfamilies, including 4 in the TST family, 4 in the vacuolar glucose transporter (VGT) family, 3 in the plastidic glucose transporter (pGlcT) family, 4 in the inositol transporters (INT) family, 31 in the polyol transporter (PLT) family, 14 in the early response to dehydration 6-like (SFP) family, 6 in the SUT family, 22 in the SWEET family, and 35 in the sugar transporters family or hexose transporter family (STP) (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM5">18</a>). Phylogenetic analysis of those sugar transporters showed gene family expansion in the STP and PLT families compared with sorghum (22 in STP and 17 in PLT), rice (21 in STP and 11 in PLT) and <i>Arabidopsis</i> (14 in STP and 9 in PLT) (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">15</a>). Tandem duplication analysis indicated that 19 and 23 genes of STP and PLT, respectively, could be assigned to tandem duplication, compared with 11 and 9 genes in sorghum. The cause of STP and PLT family expansions in <i>S. spontaneum</i> is tandem duplication.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec10">Disease resistance genes</h3><p><i>S. spontaneum</i> contributed disease resistance genes to modern sugarcane hybrid cultivars. We identified 361 sequences putatively encoding nucleotide-binding site (NBS) proteins, including 22 N-type, 169 NL-type, 68 CN-type and 102 CNL-type. The number of NBS-encoding genes is larger than that in sorghum<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 50" title="Cheng, X. et al. A genomic analysis of disease-resistance genes encoding nucleotide binding sites in Sorghum bicolor. Genet. Mol. Biol. 33, 292‚Äì297 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR50" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3330">50</a></sup>, owing to the species-specific tandem duplication in <i>S. spontaneum</i>. Surprisingly, 80% of the NBS-encoding genes located in the four rearrangement chromosomes (SsChr02, SsChr05, SsChr06 and SsChr07) and 51% of those were in the rearranged regions, including SsChr5 (Sb05S) 57.6‚Äì89.1 Mbp, SsChr6 (Sb05L) 54.6‚Äì90.6 Mbp, SsChr7 (Sb08S) 62.0‚Äì83.3 Mbp, SsChr2 (Sb08L) 98.5‚Äì125.9‚ÄâMbp (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">19</a>). Resistance genes are seven times more likely to locate in the four rearranged regions than in other chromosomes or regions (<i>P</i>‚Äâ&lt;‚Äâ2.2 √ó 10<sup>16</sup>, Fisher‚Äôs exact test; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">20</a>).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec11">
                           <i>S. spontaneum</i> fraction in hybrid sugarcane cultivars</h3><p>Modern sugarcane cultivars are the product of complex and repeated hybridization between <i>S. officinarum</i> and <i>S. spontaneum</i>, resulting in complex hybrids with chromosome numbers and morphologies that differ from those of their progenitors. Previous studies estimated that the <i>S. spontaneum</i> genome contributed approximately 10‚Äì20% of the modern hybrid sugarcane genome. In the modern hybrid sugarcane SP80-3280, approximately 12.25% of sequences are contributed by <i>S. spontaneum</i>. We mapped the sequences back to the AP85-441 genome, and they were randomly and evenly distributed, not constituting a set or sets of chromosomes as expected (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#Fig4">4</a>). Analysis of the integrated <i>S. spontaneum</i> fraction in 15 resequenced hybrid genomes also yielded random distribution throughout the genome (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">16</a>).</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-4" data-title="Distribution of the regions that contributed to the hybrid sugarcane genome in AP85-441."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig4" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 4: Distribution of the regions that contributed to the hybrid sugarcane
