u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2/figures/4" data-track-dest="link:Figure4 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec12">Origin and genetic diversity of <i>S. spontaneum</i>
                        </h3><p>Most of the genetic diversity found within <i>S. spontaneum</i> has not been introgressed into commercial sugarcane, and in principle, this germplasm represents a rich source of desirable agronomic traits related to stress tolerance and biomass accumulation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 51" title="Wang, L.-P. et al. Evaluation of sugarcane‚Äâ√ó‚ÄâSaccharum spontaneum progeny for biomass composition and yield components. Crop Sci. 48, 951‚Äì961 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR51" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3417">51</a></sup>. <i>S. spontaneum</i> has a broad natural range extending throughout Asia, the Indian subcontinent, the Mediterranean and Africa<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 52" title="Panje, R. &amp; Babu, C. Studies in Saccharum spontaneum distribution and geographical association of chromosome numbers. Cytologia 25, 152‚Äì172 (1960)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR52" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3424">52</a></sup>, and natural populations display a wide range of phenotypic, genetic and ploidy-level diversity.</p><p>In practice, however, nucleotide diversity (<i>œÄ</i>) across <i>S. spontaneum</i> was estimated to be 0.00021‚Äâ¬±‚Äâ0.000002 (Supplementary Tables <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">21</a> and <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">22</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">17</a>), much lower than that in other clonally propagated crops such as potato<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 53" title="Hardigan, M. A. et al. Genome diversity of tuber-bearing Solanum uncovers complex evolutionary history and targets of domestication in the cultivated potato. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 114, E9999‚ÄìE10008 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR53" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3447">53</a></sup>, cassava<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 54" title="Bredeson, J. V. et al. Sequencing wild and cultivated cassava and related species reveals extensive interspecific hybridization and genetic diversity. Nat. Biotechnol. 34, 562 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR54" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3451">54</a></sup>, grape<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 55" title="Myles, S. et al. Genetic structure and domestication history of the grape. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 3530‚Äì3535 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR55" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3455">55</a></sup> and citrus<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 56" title="Wu, G. A. et al. Genomics of the origin and evolution of Citrus. Nature 554, 311‚Äì316 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR56" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3459">56</a></sup>. We resequenced 64 diverse <i>S. spontaneum</i> accessions from the world germplasm collection, identifying 4.48 million high-confidence variants that included 3,961,408 SNPs, 201,854 insertions and 291,346 deletions, averaging 1.52 variants per kb. We identified 671,265 variants (15%) in genic regions, including 41,960 synonymous, 101,826 nonsynonymous and 491,493 intronic variants.</p><p>Both principal component analysis (PCA) and admixture-based analyses clustered the 64 <i>S. spontaneum</i> accessions into three distinct groups (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#Fig5">5a,c</a>; for other <i>K</i>-values, see Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">18a,b</a>) that were also supported by phylogenetic relationships among the 64 accessions inferred by bootstrapping and geographic origins (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">23</a>), with group 1 originating from China, the Philippines, Indonesia and Papua New Guinea and groups 2 and 3 originating from India, Pakistan and Iran. The regions of Pan-Malaysia might be the ancient hybrid zones among three groups. Ploidy varies widely within the three groups, from 6<i>x</i> to 16<i>x</i>. By mapping the ploidy levels on the bootstrapped tree (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#Fig5">5b</a>), the topology shows that the accessions of different ploidy levels (from hexaploid to hexadecaploid) diverged independently from ancestors in three groups, suggesting that the fluid ploidy levels may have independently evolved from ancestral progenitors.</p><div class="c-article-section__figure js-c-reading-companion-figures-item" data-test="figure" data-container-section="figure" id="figure-5" data-title="Population genetic structure and phylogenetic relationships among 64 S. spontaneum accessions."><figure><figcaption><b id="Fig5" class="c-article-section__figure-caption" data-test="figure-caption-text">Fig. 5: Population genetic structure and phylogenetic relationships among 64 <i>S. spontaneum</i> accessions.</b></figcaption><div class="c-article-section__figure-content"><div class="c-article-section__figure-item"><a class="c-article-section__figure-link" data-test="img-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="image" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2/figures/5" rel="nofollow"><picture><source type="image/webp" srcset="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0237-2/MediaObjects/41588_2018_237_Fig5_HTML.png?as=webp"></source><img aria-describedby="figure-5-desc" src="//media.springernature.com/lw685/springer-static/image/art%3A10.1038%2Fs41588-018-0237-2/MediaObjects/41588_2018_237_Fig5_HTML.png" alt="figure5" loading="lazy" width="685" height="546" /></picture></a></div><div class="c-article-section__figure-description" data-test="bottom-caption" id="figure-5-desc"><p><b>a</b>, Principal components (PCs) of accession variation. <b>b</b>, Bootstrapped tree of 64 <i>S</i>. <i>spontaneum</i> accessions based on genetic distance. Color bars indicate accessions with different ploidy levels (P6, hexaploid; P7, heptaploid; P8, octoploid; P9, enneaploid; P10, decaploid; P11‚Äì16, from hendecaploid to hexadecaploid). The scale bar shows substitutions per site. <b>c</b>, ADMIXTURE plot for <i>S</i>. <i>spontaneum</i>, showing the distribution of <i>K</i>‚Äâ=‚Äâ3 genetic clusters with the smallest cross-validation error.</p></div></div><div class="u-text-right u-hide-print"><a class="c-article__pill-button" data-test="article-link" data-track="click" data-track-label="button" data-track-action="view figure" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2/figures/5" data-track-dest="link:Figure5 Full size image" rel="nofollow"><span>Full size image</span><svg width="16" height="16" class="u-icon"><use xmlns:xlink="http://www.w3.org/1999/xlink" xlink:href="#global-icon-chevron-right"></use></svg></a></div></figure></div><p>Regions of <i>S. spontaneum</i> with larger-scale chromosomal rearrangements compared with sorghum have higher genetic diversity (higher <i>œÄ</i> value) than non-rearranged regions and may have undergone much stronger balancing selection (Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">22</a> and Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">19</a>). Although several individual chromosomes do not show significant differences, comparisons averaging values on all chromosomes show nucleotide diversity (<i>œÄ</i>) in rearranged regions (0.00025‚Äâ¬±‚Äâ0.00003) to be much higher than in non-rearranged regions (0.00021‚Äâ¬±‚Äâ0.00001, <i>P</i>‚Äâ=‚Äâ0.000234). The Tajima‚Äôs <i>D</i> in rearranged regions (‚àí0.659‚Äâ¬±‚Äâ0.052) is much higher than in non-rearranged regions (‚àí0.720‚Äâ¬±‚Äâ0.011, <i>P</i>‚Äâ=‚Äâ0.005013). SNP density is also higher in rearranged regions (360.27‚Äâ¬±‚Äâ48.41) than in non-rearranged regions (297.46‚Äâ¬±‚Äâ12.65, <i>P</i>‚Äâ=‚Äâ0.001798). In addition, the GO enrichment analyses showed that the non-rearranged regions are enriched in GOs related to basic life cycles, primarily in photosynthesis, respiration and ATP synthesis (both FDR and <i>P</i>‚Äâ&lt;‚Äâ0.05, Fisher‚Äôs exact test; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">23</a>), whereas the rearranged regions were enriched in many GOs related to secondary life processes, whole celluar process and intracellular anabolic and catabolic processes, transmembrane transport and ion binding (both FDR and <i>P</i>‚Äâ&lt;‚Äâ0.05, Fisher‚Äôs exact test; Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">24</a>).</p><p>An intriguing question is whether genomic rearranged regions might have had a role in adaptation to different habitats. The rearranged regions in <i>S. spontaneum</i> with high levels of genomic diversity (<i>œÄ</i> value) might result from a preponderance of adaptive genes related to habitat or stress adaptation, such as responses to various abiotic stresses (drought, salinity, alkaline, metal ions and so on), which are controlled by genes of whole celluar process and intracellular anabolic and catabolic processes, transmembrane transport and ion binding, as detected in these regions. Conversely, it is also meaningful that the non-rearranged regions mainly controlling basic life cycles maintain a lower level of genomic diversity. The rearranged regions have undergone stronger balancing selection after the polyploidization events. Adding further support to this notion is that 80% of the NBS-encoding genes are located on the four rearrangement chromosomes.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec13" data-title="Discussion"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec13-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec13">Discussion</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec13-content"><p>The identification of 80% of disease resistance genes on rearranged chromosomes suggests that reduction of basic chromosome number might have contributed to the retention of disease-resistance genes. Following chromosome fissions and translocations in a diploid ancestor, translocated fragments may have undergone little recombination. Following WGD, additional chromosomal rearrangements in these translocated regions may have further suppressed recombination (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#Fig1">1</a>). Population genomic analyses detected balancing selection in these rearranged regions, a mechanism to maintain genetic diversity. It is likely an unintended consequence that these rearranged chromosome arms are enriched with NBS-encoding genes, resulting in more disease-resistance genes being retained in <i>S. spontaneum</i>, which leads to higher resistance to disease and abiotic stresses in <i>S. spontaneum</i> than in other <i>Saccharum</i> species and makes <i>S. spontaneum</i> the source of disease and stress tolerance in sugarcane breeding program.</p><p>Integration of <i>S. spontaneum</i> chromosome segments into modern sugarcane hybrid cultivars by three to four generations of backcrossing at random would result in about one set of monoploid <i>S. spontaneum</i> chromosomes. The <i>S. spontaneum</i> fraction of the sugarcane hybrid cultivar SP80-3280 and of 15 resequenced hybrid genomes each appear randomly distributed in the reference AP85-441 genome, indicating random recombination of homologous chromosomes in different accessions that have undergone many rounds of meiosis after their separation. This is indirect evidence that <i>S. spontaneum</i> is autopolyploid, and it reinforces the importance of allele-specific annotation for mining effective alleles of resistance genes in hybrid cultivars.</p><p>Defining alleles in an autopolyploid genome clarifies gene or gene family analysis, as demonstrated in P450 and other gene families. This reference genome offers substantial new knowledge and unprecedented genomic resources for sugarcane breeders and researchers to mine disease resistance and other alleles in rearranged chromosomes from historic hybrid cultivars, and to track them in breeding populations to shorten the 13-year breeding cycle.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec14">URLs</h3><p>FGENESH online version, <a href="http://www.softberry.com/berry.phtml?topic=fgenesh&amp;group=help&amp;subgroup=gfind">http://www.softberry.com/berry.phtml?topic=fgenesh&amp;group=help&amp;subgroup=gfind</a>; FigTree, <a href="http://tree.bio.ed.ac.uk/software/figtree/">http://tree.bio.ed.ac.uk/software/figtree/</a>; National Center for Biotechnology Information non-redundant (NCBI NR) database for <i>Oryza sativa</i>, <a href="https://ftp.ncbi.nih.gov/blast/db">ftp://ftp.ncbi.nih.gov/blast/db</a>; RepeatModeler, <a href="http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/">http://www.repeatmasker.org/RepeatModeler/</a>.</p></div></div></section><section aria-labelledby="Sec15" data-title="Methods"><div class="c-article-section" id="Sec15-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="Sec15">Methods</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="Sec15-content"><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec16">Genome sequencing</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec17">Illumina short reads sequencing</h4><p>DNA was extracted from leaf tissue of a single soil-grown plant using the Qiagen DNeasy Plant Mini Kit and applied to 280-bp and 500-bp paired-end library construction using the NEBNext Ultra DNA Library Prep Kit for Illumina sequencing. Sequencing was performed using the Illumina HiSeq 2500 platform.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec18">Construction of BAC libraries and sequencing</h4><p>Nuclei were isolated from the young leaf tissues of AP85-441 following the method described by Ming et al.<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 57" title="Ming, R. et al. Construction and characterization of a papaya BAC library as a foundation for molecular dissection of a tree-fruit genome. Theor. Appl. Genet. 102, 892‚Äì899 (2001)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR57" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3701">57</a></sup>. The high-molecular-weight DNA embedded in agarose was partially digested using HindIII. Fractions at approximately 100‚Äâkb were recovered and cloned into the pSMART BAC vector (Lucigen). A total of 38,400 BAC clones were constructed and selected for sequencing; 48 BAC clones were pooled together, and DNA libraries were prepared with the PhasePrep BAC DNA Kit (Sigma) following the manufacturer‚Äôs protocols. BAC DNA libraries were sequenced using the Illumina HiSeq 2500 with a 250-bp paired-end sequencing strategy.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec19">PacBio library construction and sequencing</h4><p>More than 5‚Äâ¬µg of sheared and concentrated DNA was applied to size selection by the BluePippin system. Approximately 20-kb SMRTbell libraries were prepared according to the released protocol from PacBio. A total of 176 Single-Molecule, Real-Time (SMRT) cells were run on the PacBio RS II system with P6-C4 chemistry.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec20">Hi-C library construction and sequencing</h4><p>Four Hi-C libraries were created from tender leaves of AP85-441 at BioMarker Technologies Company as described previously<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 58" title="Xie, T. et al. De novo plant genome assembly based on chromatin interactions: a case study of Arabidopsis thaliana. Mol. Plant 8, 489‚Äì492 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR58" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3721">58</a></sup>. Briefly, the leaves were fixed with formaldehyde and lysed, and then the cross-linked DNA was digested with HindIII overnight. Sticky ends were biotinylated and proximity-ligated to form chimeric junctions that were enriched for and then physically sheared to a size of 500‚Äì700‚Äâbp. Chimeric fragments representing the original cross-linked long-distance physical interactions were then processed into paired-end sequencing libraries, and 1,001 million 150-bp paired-end reads were produced on the Illumina HiSeq X Ten platform.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec21">Genome assembly overview</h3><p>The sugarcane AP85-441 contig-level assembly incorporated sequencing data from a mixture of sequencing technologies (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">1</a>), including BAC pools sequenced with Illumina HiSeq 2500 and whole-genome shotgun sequencing with PacBio RS II as well as Hi-C reads, followed by Illumina short reads polishing. Each BAC pool was independently assembled using ALLPATHS-LG<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 8" title="Gnerre, S. et al. High-quality draft assemblies of mammalian genomes from massively parallel sequence data. Proc. Natl. Acad. Sci. USA 108, 1513‚Äì1518 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR8" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3737">8</a></sup>, SPAdes<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 9" title="Bankevich, A. et al. SPAdes: a new genome assembly algorithm and its applications to single-cell sequencing. J. Comput. Biol. 19, 455‚Äì477 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR9" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3741">9</a></sup> and SOAPdenovo2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 10" title="Luo, R. et al. SOAPdenovo2: an empirically improved memory-efficient short-read de novo assembler. GigaScience 1, 18 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR10" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3745">10</a></sup>, and best results were retained. For PacBio assembly, Canu v1.5<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 11" title="Koren, S. et al. Canu: scalable and accurate long-read assembly via adaptive k-mer weighting and repeat separation. Genome Res. 27, 722‚Äì736 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR11" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3749">11</a></sup> was used, as it is capable of avoiding collapsed repetitive regions and haplotypes. Self-correction was performed with parameter corOutCoverage‚Äâ=‚Äâ100, which allowed us to correct all of the input PacBio reads. The corrected reads, along with BAC-assembled contigs, were imported to the assembly step. Chromosomal assembly was constructed based on proximity-guided assembly using our newly developed program, ALLHIC, which is designed for polyploid genome scaffolding (see <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">Supplementary Note</a> for details).</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec22">Genome annotation</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec23">Repeat prediction</h4><p>We first customized a de novo repeat library of the genome using RepeatModeler (see URLs), which can automatically execute two de novo repeat finding programs, including RECON (version 1.08)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 59" title="Bao, Z. &amp; Eddy, S. R. Automated de novo identification of repeat sequence families in sequenced genomes. Genome Res. 12, 1269‚Äì1276 (2002)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR59" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3769">59</a></sup> and RepeatScout (version 1.0.5)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 60" title="Price, A. L., Jones, N. C. &amp; Pevzner, P. A. De novo identification of repeat families in large genomes. Bioinformatics 21(Suppl. 1), i351‚Äìi358 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR60" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3773">60</a></sup>. The consensus transposable element (TE) sequences generated above were imported to RepeatMasker (version 4.05)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 61" title="Smit, A., Hubley, R. &amp; Green, P. RepeatMasker Open-4.0, &#xA;                    http://www.repeatmasker.org&#xA;                    &#xA;                  ." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR61" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3777">61</a></sup> to identify and cluster repetitive elements. Unknown TEs were further classified using TEclass (version 2.1.3)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 62" title="Abrusan, G., Grundmann, N., DeMester, L. &amp; Makalowski, W. TEclass‚Äîa tool for automated classification of unknown eukaryotic transposable elements. Bioinformatics 25, 1329‚Äì1330 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR62" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3781">62</a></sup>. To identify tandem repeats within the genome, the Tandem Repeat Finder (TRF) package (version 4.07)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 63" title="Benson, G. Tandem repeats finder: a program to analyze DNA sequences. Nucleic Acids Res. 27, 573‚Äì580 (1999)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR63" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3785">63</a></sup> was used with the modified parameters of ‚Äò1 1 2 80 5 200 2,000 ‚Äìd ‚Äìh‚Äô to find high-order repeats. Telomeres and centromeres were identified based on the .dat output files above. Repeat sequences with more than ten monomers ‚ÄòAAACCT‚Äô were identified as telomeres. For centromere identification, we used a similar method described in the <i>Oropetium thomaeum</i> genome<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 64" title="VanBuren, R. et al. Single-molecule sequencing of the desiccation-tolerant grass Oropetium thomaeum. Nature 527, 508‚Äì511 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR64" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3793">64</a></sup>. The largest repeat arrays were identified and clustered as centromeres. To further investigate LTRs, we applied the LTR_retriever pipeline<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Ou, S. &amp; Jiang, N. LTR_retriever: a highly accurate and sensitive program for identification of LTR retrotransposons. Plant Physiol. 176, 1410‚Äì1422 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3797">65</a></sup>, which can integrate results from public programs such as LTR_FINDER<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 66" title="Xu, Z. &amp; Wang, H. LTR_FINDER: an efficient tool for the prediction of full-length LTR retrotransposons. Nucleic Acids Res. 35, W265‚ÄìW268 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR66" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3801">66</a></sup> and LTRharvest<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 67" title="Ellinghaus, D., Kurtz, S. &amp; Willhoeft, U. LTRharvest, an efficient and flexible software for de novo detection of LTR retrotransposons. BMC Bioinformatics 9, 18 (2008)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR67" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3805">67</a></sup> and efficiently remove false positives from the initial predictions. The predicted LTRs were further classified into intact and non-intact LTRs, and the insertion time was estimated as <i>T</i>‚Äâ=‚Äâ<i>K</i>/2<i>Œº</i> (<i>K</i> is the divergence rate, and <i>Œº</i> is the neutral mutation rate; the default is 1.38‚Äâ√ó‚Äâ10<sup>‚àí8</sup> in LTR_retriever) using the scripts implemented in the LTR_retriever package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 65" title="Ou, S. &amp; Jiang, N. LTR_retriever: a highly accurate and sensitive program for identification of LTR retrotransposons. Plant Physiol. 176, 1410‚Äì1422 (2018)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR65" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3827">65</a></sup>.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec24">Gene annotation</h4><p>To get high-quality annotation of protein-coding genes, we carried out two rounds of MAKER running, following extensive and careful manual inspections in JBrowse<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Buels, R. et al. JBrowse: a dynamic web platform for genome visualization and analysis. Genome Biol. 17, 66 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3839">68</a></sup>.</p><p>In the first round of MAKER running, ten selected RNA sequencing (RNA-seq) samples were imported into Trinity de novo assembly and genome-guided assembly pipelines with default parameters<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 69" title="Haas, B. J. et al. De novo transcript sequence reconstruction from RNA-seq using the Trinity platform for reference generation and analysis. Nat. Protoc. 8, 1494‚Äì1512 (2013)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR69" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3846">69</a></sup>. RSEM was used to calculate transcript abundance<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 70" title="Li, B. &amp; Dewey, C. N. RSEM: accurate transcript quantification from RNA-Seq data with or without a reference genome. BMC Bioinformatics 12, 323 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR70" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3850">70</a></sup>. Transcripts with FPKM (fragments per kilobase of exon per million fragments mapped)‚Äâ&lt;‚Äâ1 and iso-percentage‚Äâ&lt;‚Äâ3% were removed from further analysis. The filtered transcripts were imported to the PASA program for construction of comprehensive transcripts, as PASA is able to take advantage of the high sensitivity of reference-based assembly while leveraging the ability of de novo assembly to detect novel transcripts<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 71" title="Haas, B. J. et al. Improving the Arabidopsis genome annotation using maximal transcript alignment assemblies. Nucleic Acids Res. 21, 5654‚Äì5666 (2003)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR71" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3854">71</a></sup>. The PASA-assembled transcripts described above were used for training. The nearly ‚Äòfull-length‚Äô transcripts were evaluated by comparing with the UniProt plant protein database (last accessed on 8 December 2016), and proteins that were covered at least 95% were retained as candidates. Then ab initio gene predictors, including SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3858">72</a></sup>, GENEMARK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Lomsadze, A., Ter-Hovhannisyan, V., Chernoff, Y. O. &amp; Borodovsky, M. Gene identification in novel eukaryotic genomes by self-training algorithm. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, 6494‚Äì6506 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3862">73</a></sup> and AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Stanke, M., Schoffmann, O., Morgenstern, B. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction in eukaryotes with a generalized hidden Markov model that uses hints from external sources. BMC Bioinformatics 7, 62 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3867">74</a></sup>, were each trained with those selected proteins. After that, the MAKER pipeline was used to integrate multiple tiers of coding evidence, including ab initio gene prediction, transcript evidence and protein evidence and generate a comprehensive set of protein-coding genes.</p><p>In the second round of MAKER running, the predicted gene models with AED score equal to 0 were extracted for retraining using SNAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 72" title="Korf, I. Gene finding in novel genomes. BMC Bioinformatics 5, 59 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR72" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3874">72</a></sup>, GENEMARK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 73" title="Lomsadze, A., Ter-Hovhannisyan, V., Chernoff, Y. O. &amp; Borodovsky, M. Gene identification in novel eukaryotic genomes by self-training algorithm. Nucleic Acids Res. 33, 6494‚Äì6506 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR73" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3878">73</a></sup> and AUGUSTUS<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 74" title="Stanke, M., Schoffmann, O., Morgenstern, B. &amp; Waack, S. Gene prediction in eukaryotes with a generalized hidden Markov model that uses hints from external sources. BMC Bioinformatics 7, 62 (2006)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR74" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3882">74</a></sup>. In addition, the RNA-seq reads were mapped to the AP85-441 genome using HiSAT2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Kim, D., Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat. Methods 12, 357‚Äì360 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3886">75</a></sup> version 2.10 and reassembled using StringTie<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Pertea, M., Kim, D., Pertea, G. M., Leek, J. T. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Transcript-level expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with HISAT, StringTie and Ballgown. Nat. Protoc. 11, 1650‚Äì1667 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3890">76</a></sup> version 1.3.4, which is a reference-based RNA assembler. Meanwhile, published full-length transcripts based on IsoSeq in sugarcane were also recruited for annotation<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 77" title="Hoang, N. V. et al. A survey of the complex transcriptome from the highly polyploid sugarcane genome using full-length isoform sequencing and de novo assembly from short read sequencing. BMC Genomics 18, 395 (2017)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR77" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3895">77</a></sup>. The four haplotypes (A, B, C and D) were split into four sub-genomes, each containing eight pseudo-molecules.</p><p>Gene structures were visualized in JBrowse<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 68" title="Buels, R. et al. JBrowse: a dynamic web platform for genome visualization and analysis. Genome Biol. 17, 66 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR68" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3902">68</a></sup> along with RNA-seq-assembled transcripts and homologs from the sorghum, maize and rice genomes. We compared the two rounds of MAKER annotation and selected the better ones if their structures were better supported by homologous proteins or RNA-seq-assembled transcripts. Genes in the first round of annotation were kept if their structures did not improve significantly in the second round.</p><p>Extensive manual inspection of the annotation identified that 28,306 gene models had a significant difference in protein length or sequence similarity compared with other reported protein sequences in the NCBI NR database. The corresponding genomic DNA sequences of these genes were extracted for further careful annotation using the online version of FGENESH (see URLs) with pretrained parameters for sorghum genes. Genes with significant improvement were replaced with the FGENESH annotation.</p><p>BUSCO<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 20" title="Simao, F. A., Waterhouse, R. M., Ioannidis, P., Kriventseva, E. V. &amp; Zdobnov, E. M. BUSCO: assessing genome assembly and annotation completeness with single-copy orthologs. Bioinformatics 31, 3210‚Äì3212 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR20" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3913">20</a></sup> version 3 was used for evaluation of annotation completeness. Out of 1,440 conserved genes, 1,397 (97.1%) were re-annotated in the AP85-441 genome, among which 1,101 (76.5%) were complete and duplicated BUSCO genes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec25">Allelic variation analysis</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec26">Construction of a monoploid genome</h4><p>To compare the allelic variations among the four haplotypes, we first generated a monoploid genome. The concept of the monoploid genome is aimed at retaining consensus sequences among four haplotypes and covering as many genetic materials as possible. The longest pseudo-molecule was used as reference for each set of haplotypes, and the other three haplotypes were mapped against the reference for SNP/indel and SV calling using the nucmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3930">78</a></sup> program. Mapping results were filtered, and only the best hits were retained. The program show-snps, implemented in the MUMmer package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3934">78</a></sup>, was used to identify SNPs and indels with parameters ‚ÄìClr, which means only SNPs/indels from ambiguous mapping were reported. Consensus sequences were extracted using a homemade PERL script. Insertions larger than 50‚Äâbp were identified on Assemblytics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Nattestad, M. &amp; Schatz, M. C. Assemblytics: a web analytics tool for the detection of variants from an assembly. Bioinformatics 32, 3021‚Äì3023 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3938">79</a></sup>, a Web-based SV analytics tool, and further inserted into the reference genome. Finally, a monoploid genome, containing eight representative pseudomolecules and 785‚ÄâMbp of sequence, was generated for further analysis (Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="figure anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#Fig3">3</a>).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec27">Identification of alleles</h4><p>Identification of alleles in the AP85-441 genome was based on two strategies: (1) synteny-based and (2) coordinate-based approaches. Interhaplotype syntenic blocks were identified by MCScanX<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 80" title="Wang, Y. et al. MCScanX: a toolkit for detection and evolutionary analysis of gene synteny and collinearity. Nucleic Acids Res. 40, e49 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR80" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3953">80</a></sup> and organized into a four-column table containing allele A, B, C or D. In addition, genes that were not shown in that table were mapped to the monoploid genome using GMAP<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 81" title="Wu, T. D. &amp; Watanabe, C. K. GMAP: a genomic mapping and alignment program for mRNA and EST sequences. Bioinformatics 21, 1859‚Äì1875 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR81" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3957">81</a></sup>, and those with at least 50% overlaps on coordinates were considered as potential alleles. Sequence similarities were checked among alleles on the basis of reciprocal blast, and genes without significant similarities to any other allele were removed from the table.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec28">Analysis of allelic variations</h4><p>We use a reference-based strategy to identify SNPs/indels and SVs. Similar to the approach described in the previous section, the nucmer<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 78" title="Kurtz, S. et al. Versatile and open software for comparing large genomes. Genome Biol. 5, R12 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR78" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3969">78</a></sup> program was used to map haplotypes A, B, C and D to the monoploid genome and SNPs were extracted from ambiguous best mapping. Short indels (1‚Äì10‚Äâbp) and large structural variations were recalled by Assemblytics<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 79" title="Nattestad, M. &amp; Schatz, M. C. Assemblytics: a web analytics tool for the detection of variants from an assembly. Bioinformatics 32, 3021‚Äì3023 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR79" id="ref-link-section-d3291e3973">79</a></sup> on the basis of the alignments above.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec29">Identification of the sequences in hybrid sugarcane that originate from <i>S. spontaneum</i>
                        </h3><p>The SP80-3280 genome was first masked using the customized TE library and then split into 1-kb fragments. Each of the fragments was blast against the AP85-441 and LA-purple (unpublished) masked genomes, respectively, and the mapping score was calculated for each blast hit using the following formula:</p><div id="Equa" class="c-article-equation"><div class="c-article-equation__content"><span class="mathjax-tex">$$S = N \ast I$$</span></div></div><p>where <i>S</i> indicates mapping score, <i>N</i> indicates the number of matched bases and <i>I</i> indicates identity in each blast hit.</p><p>Fragments were further classified as sequences from AP85-441 and sequences from LA-purple if they had a best mapping score in the corresponding category. Sequences were classified as fragments from both if they had similar mapping scores (&lt;5% difference) in the LA-purple and AP85-441 genomes.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec30">Allelic expression dominance</h3><p>Tissues including leaves, stems and roots were collected from mature plants, and RNA-seq analysis in this part was based on these three samples. RNA-seq reads were trimmed by Trimmomatic and then mapped to the AP85-441 genome by HiSAT2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 75" title="Kim, D., Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. HISAT: a fast spliced aligner with low memory requirements. Nat. Methods 12, 357‚Äì360 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR75" id="ref-link-section-d3291e4041">75</a></sup>. FPKM was calculated on the basis of unique mapping reads using the StringTie package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 76" title="Pertea, M., Kim, D., Pertea, G. M., Leek, J. T. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Transcript-level expression analysis of RNA-seq experiments with HISAT, StringTie and Ballgown. Nat. Protoc. 11, 1650‚Äì1667 (2016)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR76" id="ref-link-section-d3291e4045">76</a></sup>. To analyze allelic expression dominance, we extracted 4,289 genes with full of four alleles from the AP85-441 annotation files. To simplify the analysis of the allelic expression, the expression levels of the genes were sorted in descending order from I to IV. Three allele pairs (I/II, II/III and III/IV) were compared to analyze the differentially expressed alleles. Allele pairs with less than twofold difference in expression were defined as a neutral pair and all others as non-neutral.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec31">Resequencing and population analysis</h3><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec32">Reads mapping and variants calling</h4><p>The raw pair-end reads of 64 <i>S. spontaneum</i> accessions were trimmed to remove the adaptors and low-quality bases using Trimmomatic<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 82" title="Bolger, A. M., Lohse, M. &amp; Usadel, B. Trimmomatic: a flexible trimmer for Illumina sequence data. Bioinformatics 30, 2114‚Äì2120 (2014)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR82" id="ref-link-section-d3291e4064">82</a></sup> after quality control by FastQC<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 83" title="Andrews, S. FastQC, &#xA;                    https://www.bioinformatics.babraham.ac.uk/projects/fastqc/&#xA;                    &#xA;                   (Babraham Bioinformatics, 2010)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR83" id="ref-link-section-d3291e4068">83</a></sup>. The reads were filtered with a sliding window of size 7, with average Phred score scale of 20 within the window. The trimmed reads were mapped to the <i>S. spontaneum</i> genome using Bowtie2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat. Methods 9, 357‚Äì359 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d3291e4075">84</a></sup> with default parameters. The mapped reads were sorted, and duplicated reads were removed using SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078‚Äì2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d3291e4080">85</a></sup>. We estimated the rate of uniquely mapped reads outputted from both BWA<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 86" title="Li, H. &amp; Durbin, R. Fast and accurate short read alignment with Burrows‚ÄìWheeler transform. Bioinformatics 25, 1754‚Äì1760 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR86" id="ref-link-section-d3291e4084">86</a></sup> and Bowtie2<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 84" title="Langmead, B. &amp; Salzberg, S. L. Fast gapped-read alignment with Bowtie 2. Nat. Methods 9, 357‚Äì359 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR84" id="ref-link-section-d3291e4088">84</a></sup>. Bowtie2 generated ten times as many uniquely mapped reads as BWA did (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">21</a>).</p><p>The Realigner Target Creator and Indel Realigner programs from the Genome Analysis Toolkit (GATK) package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 87" title="McKenna, A. et al. The Genome Analysis Toolkit: a MapReduce framework for analyzing next-generation DNA sequencing data. Genome Res. 20, 1297‚Äì1303 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR87" id="ref-link-section-d3291e4098">87</a></sup> were used for global realignment of reads around indels from the sorted BAM files. The HaplotypeCaller was used to estimate the SNPs and indels for putative diploids using the default parameters. The HaplotypeCaller outputted 42,585,337 unfiltered variants (SNPs and indels). The distribution of calling depths (DP) of each raw variant was estimated as a criterion for variants filtering. Low depths and repetitive variants were removed from the raw VCF file if they had DP‚Äâ&lt;‚Äâ2 or DP‚Äâ&gt;‚Äâ45, minQ‚Äâ&lt;‚Äâ30. Variants with more than 15% missing data were removed. These filtering strategies reduced the raw unfiltered set of 42.59 million variants (SNPs and Indels) to the working set of 4.48 million (4,476,608) variants. SnpEff (v3.6c)<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 88" title="Cingolani, P. et al. A program for annotating and predicting the effects of single nucleotide polymorphisms, SnpEff: SNPs in the genome of Drosophila melanogaster strain w&#xA;                           1118; iso-2; iso-3. Fly 6, 80‚Äì92 (2012)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR88" id="ref-link-section-d3291e4102">88</a></sup> was used to assign variants effects on the basis of gene models from <i>S. spontaneum</i> genome annotation. The variants sites were annotated as the SNPs and Indels, as well as intergenic and genic regions (including the synonymous, nonsynonymous, intronic, upstream and downstream variants).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec33">Genome-wide genetic diversity estimation</h4><p>Population genetic statistics of SNP density, œÄ and Tajima‚Äôs <i>D</i> were calculated directly from the filtered VCF file in 1,000-kb window and 500-kb step for œÄ, and non-overlapping intervals for SNPs density and Tajima‚Äôs <i>D</i> in VCFtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Danecek, P. et al. The variant call format and VCFtools. Bioinformatics 27, 2156‚Äì2158 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d3291e4123">89</a></sup>. The high confidence 4,476,608 variant set was used for statistical estimations.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec34">PCA</h4><p>PCA was performed using the GCTA software on the filtered 4,476,608 variants. The input Plink binary files are transformed from the filtered VCFs file using VCFtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 89" title="Danecek, P. et al. The variant call format and VCFtools. Bioinformatics 27, 2156‚Äì2158 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR89" id="ref-link-section-d3291e4135">89</a></sup> and PLINK<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 90" title="Purcell, S. et al. PLINK: a tool set for whole-genome association and population-based linkage analyses. Am. J. Hum. Genet. 81, 559‚Äì575 (2007)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR90" id="ref-link-section-d3291e4139">90</a></sup>. The top three principal components were used for assigning the 64 accessions and downstream population structure analysis.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec35">Phylogeny</h4><p>Bi-allelic and polymorphic SNPs (3,969,408) were used for reconstructing the phylogenetic relationships among 64 accessions. Before tree construction, we filtered and pruned the SNPs with MAF (minor allele frequency)‚Äâ&lt;‚Äâ0.2, missing rate‚Äâ&gt;‚Äâ0.15, and LD (linkage disequilibrium) threshold‚Äâ=‚Äâ0.2. Finally, a total of 37,617 SNPs were selected for the constructed tree using SNPhylo software. The multiple consensus sequences were aligned using MUSCLE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 91" title="Edgar, R. C. MUSCLE: multiple sequence alignment with high accuracy and high throughput. Nucleic Acids Res. 32, 1792‚Äì1797 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR91" id="ref-link-section-d3291e4151">91</a></sup>. Maximum likelihood trees were constructed using the maximum likelihood method by running DNAML programs in the PHYLIP package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 92" title="Plotree, D. &amp; Plotgram, D. PHYLIP-phylogeny inference package (version 3.2). Cladistics 5, 6 (1989)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR92" id="ref-link-section-d3291e4155">92</a></sup>. In addition, a bootstrapped tree was constructed by bootstrapping (bootstrap‚Äâ=‚Äâ10,000) analysis using the PHANGORN package<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 93" title="Schliep, K. P. phangorn: phylogenetic analysis in R. Bioinformatics 27, 592 (2011)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR93" id="ref-link-section-d3291e4159">93</a></sup>. Figtree v.1.4 (see URLs) was used to visualize the trees.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec36">Population genetic structure</h4><p>Ancestral population stratification among 64 accessions was inferred using Admixture software. The optimal ancestral population structure was estimated from the same variants set with STRUCTURE<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 94" title="Evanno, G., Regnaut, S. &amp; Goudet, J. Detecting the number of clusters of individuals using the software STRUCTURE: a simulation study. Mol. Ecol. 14, 2611‚Äì2620 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR94" id="ref-link-section-d3291e4172">94</a></sup> using ancestral population sizes <i>K</i>‚Äâ=‚Äâ1‚Äì20 and choosing the population with the smallest cross-validation error. The parameter standard errors were estimated using bootstrapping (bootstrap‚Äâ=‚Äâ200) when doing the admixture analyses. DISTRUCT<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 95" title="Rosenberg, N. A. DISTRUCT: a program for the graphical display of population structure. Mol. Ecol. Res. 4, 137‚Äì138 (2004)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR95" id="ref-link-section-d3291e4179">95</a></sup> was used to plot the population stratification results for <i>K</i>‚Äâ=‚Äâ1 through <i>K</i>‚Äâ=‚Äâ20 (Supplementary Fig. <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">18</a>).</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec37">Differentiation of genomic diversity among four homologous haploid sets</h4><p>The reads mapped to each of four homologous haploid sets (A, B, C and D) of the AP85-441 genome were retrieved for each of 64 accessions using SAMtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 85" title="Li, H. et al. The sequence alignment/map format and SAMtools. Bioinformatics 25, 2078‚Äì2079 (2009)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR85" id="ref-link-section-d3291e4201">85</a></sup> and Bedtools<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 96" title="Quinlan, A. R. &amp; Hall, I. M. BEDTools: a flexible suite of utilities for comparing genomic features. Bioinformatics 26, 841‚Äì842 (2010)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR96" id="ref-link-section-d3291e4205">96</a></sup>. The four sets of retrieved reads for each of 64 accessions were mapped separately to each of eight chromosomes in a consensus monoploid genome using Bowtie2 with default parameters. The variants were called from a cohort of 256 BAM files generated from the previous step for each of the eight chromosomes. The HaplotypeCaller of GATK was used to estimate the SNPs and indels for putative diploids using the default parameters. The HaplotypeCaller outputted 17,531,765 unfiltered variants (SNPs and indels). The distribution of calling depths (DP) of each raw variant was estimated as a criterion for variant filtering. Low depths and repetitive variants were removed from the raw VCF file if they had DP‚Äâ&lt;‚Äâ1 or DP‚Äâ&gt;‚Äâ5, minQ‚Äâ&lt;‚Äâ20. We allowed the variant sites with max-missing rate as 50%. These filtering strategies reduced the raw unfiltered set of variants (SNPs and indels) to the working set of 68,911 variants. These filtering strategies reduced the raw unfiltered set of variants (SNPs and indels) to the working set of 68,911 variants. The working variant set was then used for estimating the population genetics statistics œÄ among four homologous haploid sets.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec38">Genomic diversity of genomic rearranged regions</h4><p>To test whether genomic rearranged regions (RAR) have a genetic difference from non-rearranged regions (non-RAR), we compared the population genetic statistics <i>œÄ</i>, SNP density, and Tajima‚Äôs <i>D</i> between rearranged and non-rearranged regions in each of four sets (A, B, C and D) of chromosomes 2, 5, 6 and 7. The genomic rearranged regions inferred by collinear dot plot and alleles phasing are shown Supplementary Table <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM1">21</a>. We used the <i>T</i>-test and Mann-Whitney <i>U</i> test with the one-tailed hypothesis to compare the differences of statistics (<i>œÄ</i>, SNP density and Tajima‚Äôs <i>D</i>) between RAR and non-RAR. To find the difference in gene functions between RAR and non-RAR, we conducted GO enrichment analysis for the gene models in RAR and non-RAR. We first blastX the <i>S. spontaneum</i> gene models in the NCBI NR database of <i>Oryza sativa</i> (see URLs). Then, the functional annotation and GO enrichment analyses of gene models were conducted in Blast2Go v4.1 software<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 97" title="Conesa, A. et al. Blast2GO: a universal tool for annotation, visualization and analysis in functional genomics research. Bioinformatics 21, 3674‚Äì3676 (2005)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR97" id="ref-link-section-d3291e4245">97</a></sup>. We used gene models of RAR or non-RAR as tested gene sets and the whole gene models as reference. The significance of enrichment was valued using the Fisher‚Äôs exact test.</p><h4 class="c-article__sub-heading c-article__sub-heading--small" id="Sec39">Genomic diversity among different polyploidy accessions</h4><p>To test the effects of polyploidization on genetic diversity, we compared the population nucleotide diversity (<i>œÄ</i>) among accessions with different ploidy levels. We used a 1,000-kb sliding window and a 500-kb step to calculate the values of each statistic. In addition, we divided the 64 accessions into four groups (ploidy 6, 8, 10 and 13‚Äì16) depending on their ploidy level. The four groups were used to calculate the pairwise Weir and Cockerham‚Äôs <i>F</i><sub>ST</sub> between the two of them using VCFtools version 0.1.12b<sup><a data-track="click" data-track-action="reference anchor" data-track-label="link" data-test="citation-ref" aria-label="Reference 46" title="Casu, R. E. et al. Tissue-specific transcriptome analysis within the maturing sugarcane stalk reveals spatial regulation in the expression of cellulose synthase and sucrose transporter gene families. Plant Mol. Biol. 89, 607‚Äì628 (2015)." href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#ref-CR46" id="ref-link-section-d3291e4264">46</a></sup> with a 1,000-kb sliding window and a 500-kb step.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="Sec40">Reporting Summary</h3><p>Further information on research design is available in the <a data-track="click" data-track-label="link" data-track-action="supplementary material anchor" href="/articles/s41588-018-0237-2#MOESM2">Nature Research Reporting Summary</a> linked to this article.</p></div></div></section>
                

                <section aria-labelledby="data-availability" data-title="Data availability"><div class="c-article-section" id="data-availability-section"><h2 class="c-article-section__title js-section-title js-c-reading-companion-sections-item" id="data-availability">Data availability</h2><div class="c-article-section__content" id="data-availability-content">
              
              <p>The genome assembly and gene annotation have been deposited in the NCBI database under accession number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/nuccore/QVOL00000000">QVOL00000000</a>, BioProject number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/bioproject/?term=PRJNA483885">PRJNA483885</a> and BioSample number <a href="https://www.ncbi.nlm.nih.gov/biosample/?term=SAMN09753102">SAMN09753102</a>. The data can also be downloaded from the following link: <a href="http://www.life.illinois.edu/ming/downloads/Spontaneum_genome/">http://www.life.illinois.edu/ming/downloads/Spontaneum_genome/</a>.</p>

J.Z. and Xingtan Z. constructed the linkage map; Q.Z., H.T., Y.S., S.Chen. and X.X. studied genome evolution; J.Z., X.H., X.Zhu, M.L., F.C. and G.Z. conducted the C<sub>4</sub> photosynthesis analysis; J.Z., Xingtan Z., Q.Z., X.H., Y.S., L.H., Z.Li, Y.W., W.H. and Jishan L. analyzed gene families; R.M., Xingtan Z., F.Z., J.Z., Q.Z., X.H., X.M., Y.S., S.Chen, X.X., J.Y., L.H., Z.Li, H.X., D.Zhou, Y.W., W.H., L.W., Jishan L., Y.D., P.M., Z.Y., F.D., Z.Lin, Hai L., Hong L., H.Y., W.Zhong, P.L., Gang W., Guofeng W.,Y.Y., J.Song, J.Shi, J.H., Jingxian L., Q.S., Q.J., P.Z., Yaying M., Xunxiao Z., R.X., J.Liu, Y.Z., H.J., Q.M., R.Q., Z.Z., Y.D., N.P., M.M., D.Zerpa, J.K.N., L.W., L.Y., Y.X., G.D., H.F., J.J., J.F., Z.C., Z.L., M.H., M.W., T.M., X.Y. and L.G. manually checked the gene allele annotation; S.R.D., D.M.B., S.E.H. and L.-Q.C. analyzed sugar transporters; D.N. and M.S. analyzed P450 genes; Z.Z. and K.W. analyzed chromosome centromeres; C.Z. and D.S. tested whole-genome duplications; X.M., R.V.B., C.M.W., X.Y.J., J.W., J.H.T., M.C.M. and J.J.R. analyzed resequenced populations; M.C.S., D.H., M.-A.V.S. and G.M.S. contributed the SP80-3280 genome; and R.M., A.H.P., J.Z., H.T. and Xingtan Z. wrote the manuscript.</p><h3 class="c-article__sub-heading" id="corresponding-author">Corresponding authors</h3><p id="corresponding-author-list">Correspondence to
